Ruling on Guarantee by Two Joint Debtors
Explains ruling when two debtors are mutual guarantors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for recourse when two joint debtors are mutual guarantors and apply it to calculate the reclaimable amount.

Core Idea
A paying joint debtor can only reclaim the surplus over his own half from his co-debtor-guarantor.

This passage from the Hidayah addresses a nuanced scenario in the chapter of guarantee (kafala): two persons jointly owe a debt and each guarantees the other. The ruling determines how much a paying debtor can recover from his partner. Understanding this requires distinguishing between the stronger obligation of principal (asīl) and the weaker demand (mutalaba) of guarantee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two persons are joint principal debtors (asīl) and each is also a guarantor (kafīl) for the other. Example: they jointly buy a slave for 1000 dirhams and each guarantees the other.
2
State the ruling
If one debtor pays the creditor, he cannot claim reimbursement from his partner unless he pays more than half of the total debt. He can claim only the surplus over half.
3
Understand the reasoning: obligations have different strengths
Each debtor is principal for his own half (strong obligation - dīn) and guarantor for the other half (weaker obligation - mutalaba). The debt (dīn) is a direct liability, while the guarantor's obligation is merely a demand (mutalaba).
Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between dīn and mutalaba.
4
Apply the precedence rule
When payment is made, it is first applied to the stronger obligation (the principal debt). Therefore, the first half of the payment goes to the payer’s own share, and only the excess beyond that is attributed to the guarantee.
5
Recognize the circularity argument
If the payment were attributed to the guarantee within the first half, the co-debtor could then reclaim from the payer, leading to an infinite loop (dawr). The rule avoids this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا كان الدين على اثنين وكل واحد منهما كفيل عن صاحبه كما اذا اشتريا عبداً بالف درهم وكفل كل واحد منهما عن صاحبه فما ادى احدهما لم يرجع على شريكه حتى يزيد ما يؤديه على النصف فيرجع بالزيادة لان كل واحد منهما في النصف اصيل وفى النصف الأخر كفيل ولا معارضة بين ما عليه بحق الاصالة وبحق الكفالة لان الاول دين والثانى مطالبة ثم هو تابع للاول فيقع عن الأول وفى الزيادة لامعارضة فيقع عن الكفالة ولانه لو وقع فى النصف عن صاحبه فيرجع عليه فلصاحبه ان يرجع لان ادا نائبه كا دائه فيودى إلى الدور
When the debt is upon two persons, and each of them is a guarantor for the other, like when they buy a slave for a thousand dirhams and each guarantees for the other, then whichever of them pays, he does not have recourse against his partner until what he pays exceeds half; then he has recourse for the excess. Because each of them is principal in half and guarantor in the other half, and there is no conflict between what is upon him by virtue of principality and by virtue of guarantee, because the first is a debt and the second a demand. Then the second is subordinate to the first, so (the payment) falls upon the first. And in the excess there is no conflict, so it falls upon the guarantee. And because if it falls upon the half for the partner, then he would have recourse against him, and then the partner would have recourse, because the payment by his deputy is like his own payment, leading to circularity.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter on guarantee (kafala), specifically when two persons are joint debtors and each guarantees the other. The ruling is that if one debtor pays the creditor, he cannot claim back from his co-debtor unless he pays more than half of the total debt. For example, if two people jointly owe 1000 dirhams, and each is a guarantor for the other, then if one pays 600 dirhams, he can claim 100 dirhams (the excess over 500) from his partner. The reasoning: each debtor is principal (asil) for his own half (500) and guarantor (kafil) for the other half. The debt (dīn) owed as principal is stronger than the mere demand (mutalaba) arising from guarantee. Therefore, any payment is first applied to the stronger obligation—the principal debt. Only payments exceeding the principal's share are attributed to the guarantee, and then the payer can reclaim that surplus from the co-debtor. If the payment were attributed to the guarantee within the half, it would lead to circularity: the co-debtor would reclaim from the payer, and then the payer would reclaim again, ad infinitum.

Key Terms
اصيل (asīl): The principal debtor who is primarily liable for the debt.
كفيل (kafīl): The guarantor who is secondarily liable, i.e., the creditor can demand payment from him if the principal defaults.
مطالبة (muṭālabah): The right of the creditor to demand payment from the guarantor, as opposed to the actual debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two persons jointly owe 1000 dirhams and each is a guarantor for the other. If one pays 600 dirhams, how much can he claim from his partner?
A) 0 dirhams
B) 100 dirhams
C) 300 dirhams
D) 600 dirhams

Key Takeaway
When two joint debtors are mutual guarantors, a paying debtor can only recover the surplus over his own half, because the principal debt obligation is stronger than the guarantee demand.

Mutual Guarantors: Ruling and Rationale
Explaining ruling and rationale for mutual guarantors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when one co-guarantor pays the full debt in a mutual guarantee scenario and explain why circularity (dawr) does not occur.

Core Idea
When two are mutual guarantors, the paying partner recovers half; the other cannot recover back – equality prevents circularity.

This lesson examines a nuanced case in the chapter of Kafalah: two people act as co-guarantors for a debt, and each also guarantees the other. When one pays the entire debt, what are his rights of recourse? The ruling, as explained by al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah, is that he can recover half from his co-guarantor, but the co-guarantor cannot then recover from him. The key lies in understanding that both obligations are equally due to guarantee, and allowing further recourse would disrupt the equality between them. The passage also provides a rational proof (dalil aqli) to justify this position.

Guided Path
1
State the Scenario
Identify the three parties: a debtor (asil), and two co-guarantors (Khalid and Hamid) who are each also guarantors for the other. The debt is 200 dirhams.
This is known as kafalah min al-kafil – a guarantee from a guarantor.
2
Identify the Ruling
When Hamid pays the full 200 dirhams to the creditor, he can recover 100 dirhams (half) from his co-guarantor Khalid. Khalid cannot then recover any amount from Hamid.
This is the sahih qawl (correct statement) per al-Hidayah.
3
Understand the Double Guarantee Structure
Each guarantor is both a guarantor for the debtor (kafalah ‘an al-asil) and a guarantor for his partner (kafalah min al-kafil). Therefore, the payment is apportioned: half on his own behalf and half on behalf of his partner.
The payment is considered made jointly on behalf of both guarantors.
4
Analyze the Avoidance of Circularity (Dawr)
If the co-guarantor (Khalid) from whom half was recovered could then recover from Hamid, each would end up paying unequal amounts (150 vs. 50). Since both are equal guarantors, this would break equality. Therefore, Khalid has no further recourse.
Equality (musawat) is the operative principle preventing circularity.
5
Note the Additional Options
According to al-Quduri, the paying guarantor has a choice: either recover half from the co-guarantor (then both recover from the debtor), or recover the entire 200 dirhams directly from the debtor.
This flexibility is because he is a guarantor for the debtor by his command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کتاب الکفاله
اشرف البدایہ جلد نہم

اور خالد، حامد کی طرف سے بھی کفیل ہے۔ پس اگر حامد نے مکفول لہ کو شاید[؟] کی طرف سے دو سو درہم ادا کر دئیے تو حامد کو اپنے ساتھی یعنی خالد سے اس کا نصف یعنی ایک سو درہم واپس لینے کا اختیار حاصل ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ صحیح قول کے مطابق اس مسئلہ کی مراد یہ ہے کہ کفیلین میں سے ہر ایک اصیل یعنی مکفول عنہ کی طرف سے بھی پورے مال کا کفیل ہے اور اپنے ساتھی کی طرف سے بھی پورے مال کا کفیل ہے۔ اور مطالبہ متعدد ہے اس طور پر کہ کفیلین میں سے ہر ایک نے ایک تو اس مطالبہ کا التزام کیا ہے جو مکفول لہ کی طرف سے مکفول عنہ یعنی مدیون پر تھا اور دوسرے اُس مطالبہ کا التزام کیا ہے جو کفالہ کی وجہ سے کفیل پر تھا پس کفیلین میں سے ہر ایک پر دو، دو کفالے جمع ہو جائیں گے۔

اور کفالہ کا موجب چونکہ التزام مطالبہ ہے اور کفیل پر بھی مکفول لہ کے لئے مطالبہ ہوتا ہے اس لئے کفیل کی طرف سے کفیل ہونا اسی طرح صحیح ہے جیسے اصیل (مدیون) کی طرف سے کفیل ہونا صحیح ہے۔ اور جیسے محتال سے ایک سو درہم کا نصف واپس لینے کا اختیار دے دیا جائے تو مساوات باقی نہ رہے گی ۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں ایک کی طرف سے ایک سو پچاس درہم ادا ہوں گے اور دوسرے کی طرف سے فقط پچاس درہم ادا ہوں گے۔ پس مساوات برخلاف پہلے مسئلہ کے کیونکہ پہلے مسئلہ میں ہر ایک نے پورے مال کا التزام بحکم کفالہ نہیں کیا ہے بلکہ نصف مال کا التزام شراء وغیرہ کی وجہ سے بالا صالت کیا ہے اور نصف کا التزام بحکم کفالت کیا ہے۔ لہذا اس مسئلہ میں اگر ادا کی ہوئی نصف رقم کو بحکم کفالہ قرار دے کر ساتھی سے رجوع کا اختیار دیا جائے تو رجوعات میں تسلسل لازم آئے گا۔ جیسا کہ اسکی تفصیل گذشتہ مسئلہ کی دوسری دلیل کے تحت گذر چکی ہے۔

پھر دونوں کفیل ادا ہوا مال اپنے اصیل یعنی مکفول عنہ سے واپس لے سکتے ہیں کیونکہ مکفول عنہ کی طرف سے ادا کرنے والے دونوں کفیل ہیں۔ بایں طور کہ ایک نے بذات خود ادا کیا ہے اور دوسرے کفیل نے اپنے نائب کے ذریعہ ادا کیا ہے اور نائب کا ادا کرنا چونکہ ایسا ہے جیسے بذات خود ادا کیا ہو۔ اس لئے گویا ہر ایک نے بذات خود مکفول عنہ کی طرف سے ادا کیا ہے اور جب دونوں کفیلوں نے مکفول عنہ کی طرف سے ادا کیا ہے تو دونوں کو مکفول عنہ سے رجوع کا اختیار حاصل ہوگا۔

صاحب قدوری فرماتے ہیں کہ جس کفیل نے مکفول لہ کو دو سو درہم ادا کئے ہیں اس کو جس طرح یہ اختیار ہے کہ وہ انصف (ایک سو درہم ) اپنے ساتھی یعنی دوسرے کفیل سے واپس لے اسی طرح یہ بھی اختیار ہے کہ وہ پورا مال یعنی دو سو درہم مکفول عنہ سے واپس لے۔ کیونکہ یہ مکفول عنہ کی طرف سے بھی اس کے حکم سے پورے مال کا کفیل ہے۔ لہذا جو کچھ ادا کیا ہے وہ سب مکفول عنہ سے واپس لے سکتا ہے۔ خلاصہ یہ ہوا کہ ایسے دو کفیلوں میں سے ایک کفیل نے اگر مال ادا کیا تو اس کو اختیار ہے چاہے مکفول عنہ سے پورا مال واپس لے اور چاہے کفیل سے ادا کردہ کا نصف واپس لے اور پھر دونوں مل کر مکفول عنہ سے واپس لیں ۔
Book of Guarantee. Ashraf al-Hidaya, volume nine. And Khalid is also a guarantor on behalf of Hamid. So if Hamid paid 200 dirhams to the creditor on behalf of Khalid, then Hamid has the option to take back half, i.e., 100 dirhams, from his partner Khalid. The author of al-Hidaya says: According to the correct statement, the meaning of this issue is that each of the two guarantors is a guarantor for the full amount on behalf of the original debtor (principal), and also a guarantor for the full amount on behalf of his partner. And the claim is multiple: each of the two guarantors has undertaken the claim that the creditor had against the debtor, and also the claim that arose against the guarantor due to the guarantee. Thus, two guarantees accumulate upon each of the two guarantors. Kafalah from the principal - Kafalah from the guarantor. And since the cause of guarantee is the undertaking to be claimed, and the guarantor is also subject to claim by the creditor, therefore being a guarantor on behalf of a guarantor is valid just as being a guarantor on behalf of the principal (debtor) is valid. And just as a person transferring a debt (muhtal) can validly transfer onto another what he had made obligatory upon himself, likewise being a guarantor on behalf of a guarantor is valid. In any case, when it is known that each of the two guarantors is a guarantor for the full amount on behalf of the principal (debtor) and also a guarantor for the full amount on behalf of his partner (the other guarantor), then whatever one of these two guarantors pays will be paid jointly on behalf of both. For example, if one guarantor paid 200 dirhams to the creditor, these 200 dirhams will be paid on behalf of both guarantors. That is, it will be said that this guarantor paid 100 dirhams due to kafalah from the principal, and 100 dirhams due to kafalah from the guarantor. And the proof is that here the entire amount is due to guarantee: the obligation arising from kafalah from the principal is also due to guarantee (bi-haqq al-kafalah), and the obligation arising from kafalah from the guarantor is also due to guarantee. This is unlike the first issue (al-mas'alah al-ula), where the obligation of half is due to being a principal (asalah) and the obligation of half is due to guarantee. In any case, in this issue, when both obligations are due to guarantee, neither takes precedence over the other. When neither takes precedence, the paid amount (200 dirhams) is paid on behalf of both guarantors. That is, half of the amount is paid on his own behalf due to kafalah from the principal, and half is paid on behalf of his partner (the other guarantor) due to kafalah from the guarantor. And when the paid amount is paid on behalf of both, i.e., half was paid on behalf of his partner (the other guarantor), then he has the option to take back half (100 dirhams) from his partner. And circularity will not occur. Because each of the two being a guarantor for the full amount on behalf of the debtor, and each being a guarantor for the full amount on behalf of his partner, requires that their states remain equal. And when one paid 200 dirhams and then took back half (100 dirhams) from his partner, equality is achieved. Now if the one from whom 100 dirhams were taken were given the option to take back half of 100 dirhams from the first partner, equality would not remain. Because in that case, 150 dirhams would be paid on behalf of one, and only 50 dirhams on behalf of the other. Therefore, to maintain equality, the second guarantor (from whom 100 dirhams were taken) will not have the option to have recourse. And when he does not have the option to have recourse, then circularity (serial sequence) will not occur. Unlike the first issue, because in the first issue each did not undertake the entire amount by way of guarantee; rather, they undertook half the amount due to being a principal (e.g., purchase) and half due to guarantee. Hence in that issue, if the paid half amount is considered as due to guarantee and the partner is given the option to have recourse, then serial recourse would occur, as its details have passed under the second proof of the previous issue. Then both guarantors can take back the paid amount from their principal (the debtor/makful anhu), because both guarantors are payers on behalf of the debtor. In that manner, one paid directly and the other guarantor paid through his deputy, and the deputy's payment is like his own payment. So it is as if each paid on behalf of the debtor himself. And when both guarantors paid on behalf of the debtor, both have the option to have recourse against the debtor. Al-Quduri says: The guarantor who paid 200 dirhams to the creditor has the option, just as he can take back half (100 dirhams) from his partner (the other guarantor), he can also take back the entire amount (200 dirhams) from the debtor. Because he is a guarantor for the full amount on behalf of the debtor by his command. Therefore, he can take back whatever he paid from the debtor. The summary is: If one of two such guarantors paid the amount, he has the option: either take the full amount from the debtor, or take half of the paid amount from the guarantor, and then both together take from the debtor.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario in the chapter of Kafalah (guarantee): two individuals, Khalid and Hamid, are co-guarantors for a debt owed by a third party (the principal debtor). Additionally, each is also a guarantor for the other (kafalah min al-kafil). The passage explains the ruling when one of them (Hamid) pays the full debt of 200 dirhams to the creditor. The author first states the ruling: Hamid can recover half (100 dirhams) from his co-guarantor Khalid. The author then explains the reasoning through the commentary of al-Hidayah. The key point is that each guarantor is simultaneously a guarantor for the principal debtor (kafalah 'an al-asil) and a guarantor for his co-guarantor (kafalah min al-kafil). Therefore, when one pays, the payment is apportioned: half is considered paid on his own behalf (as guarantor for the principal) and half on behalf of his co-guarantor (as guarantor for him). Consequently, he can recover the half paid on behalf of the co-guarantor from that co-guarantor. The author then addresses a potential objection: why doesn't this lead to circularity (dawr), where the co-guarantor could then recover from the first? The answer is that allowing further recourse would upset the equality between the two guarantors. Since each is a guarantor for the other for the full amount, after the first recovery, the net contributions become equal (each effectively paid 100). To maintain this equality, the co-guarantor from whom the half was taken cannot have further recourse against the first. This contrasts with another case (al-mas'alah al-ula) where one party was a principal for half and a guarantor for half, which would lead to circularity. The passage then adds that both guarantors can recover from the original debtor (makful 'anhu) since each paid on his behalf. Finally, a quote from al-Quduri gives the paying guarantor an additional option: he can either recover half from the co-guarantor and then both recover from the debtor, or he can recover the full amount directly from the debtor. This illustrates the flexibility in the Hanafi fiqh of kafalah.

Key Terms
کفالہ (kafalah): A contract of guarantee where a person (kafil) undertakes to assume liability for a debt or obligation of another (asil).
کفیل (kafil): The guarantor; the person who undertakes the kafalah.
اصیل (asil): The principal debtor (madyn) on whose behalf the guarantee is given; also called makful 'anhu.
مکفول لہ (makful lahu): The creditor/beneficiary to whom the guarantee is given; the one entitled to claim.
دور (dawr): Circularity in recourse; an infinite regress where each party could claim from the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the mutual guarantee scenario, after the paying guarantor recovers half from his co-guarantor, why can't the co-guarantor then recover from the first?
A) Because the co-guarantor is not a guarantor for the first.
B) Because the first paid in cash, not by guarantee.
C) Because allowing further recourse would break the equality between the two guarantors.
D) Because the co-guarantor can only recover from the debtor.

Key Takeaway
In mutual guarantees where both obligations are equally due to guarantee, the paying guarantor recovers half from his partner, but the partner has no further recourse – equality prevents circularity.

Release of One Guarantor: Effect on the Other
Ruling on releasing one of two mutual guarantors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that releasing one guarantor does not release the debtor or reduce the liability of the other guarantor.

Core Idea
Releasing a guarantor does not release the debtor; the other guarantor remains fully liable.

When a creditor has two guarantors for a single debtor, and each guarantor is also a guarantor for the other, what happens if the creditor releases one of them? The Hanafi school, following the reasoning of Imam al-Quduri, clarifies that the release of a guarantor does not affect the principal debtor's obligation. Consequently, the remaining guarantor is still liable for the entire debt. This ruling is based on the principle that the guarantee for the debtor is independent of the co-guarantor's release.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the key facts: a debtor (asil) with two guarantors (kafilayn), each also a guarantor for the other. The creditor (rabb al-mal) releases one guarantor.
2
State the Action and Initial Question
The creditor's act of ibra' (release) extinguishes the liability of that specific guarantor. The question is: does this reduce the debt or affect the other guarantor?
3
Apply the Core Principle
Apply the Hanafi principle: 'ibra' al-kafil la yujibu bara'at al-asil' (releasing the guarantor does not necessitate releasing the debtor). The debtor remains liable for the full debt.
This principle is explicitly stated in the passage.
4
Derive the Ruling for the Other Guarantor
Since the debtor still owes the full amount, and the other guarantor is a guarantor for the debtor (not just for the released guarantor), his guarantee for the debtor remains intact. Therefore, he is liable for the entire debt.
The other guarantor's liability is not reduced to half because his guarantee for the debtor is independent.
5
Note the Practical Consequence
The creditor can claim the full debt from the unreleased guarantor. The released guarantor is free from liability, but the debtor and the other guarantor are still bound.
This ruling holds even if each guarantor was also a guarantor for the other; the release of one only extinguishes that one's personal liability.

The creditor released one guarantor; he can collect the full amount from the other. He (al-Quduri) said: When the creditor releases one of them, he can take the full amount from the other, because releasing a guarantor does not necessitate the release of the principal (debtor). So the full amount remains upon the principal, and the other is a guarantor for him for the full amount, as we have explained. And for this reason, the creditor can hold the guarantor liable for the full amount. Translation: And if the creditor released one of the two guarantors, then he can take the full debt from the other. Because releasing a guarantor does not cause the release of the principal. So the full debt remains upon the principal, and the other is a guarantor for him for the full debt, as we have explained. For this reason, the creditor can hold the guarantor liable for the full debt. Explanation: The scenario is: when two persons become guarantors on behalf of one person, and each of them is a guarantor on behalf of the other, then if the creditor releases one of these two guarantors, the creditor has the right to collect the full debt from the other guarantor. Because releasing a guarantor does not release the debtor (makful anhu), and when the debtor is not released, the full debt remains upon him. And the other guarantor is a guarantor for him for the full debt, as has passed before. And since the full debt remains upon the debtor and the other is a guarantor for him for the full debt, the creditor can demand the full debt from that guarantor who was not released from the claim of the debt.

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from al-Quduri on the effect of releasing one of two guarantors who are also mutual guarantors. The Arabic text is from al-Quduri's matn, followed by its translation and explanation in Urdu. The key point: if the creditor (rabb al-mal) releases one of the two guarantors (e.g., Khalid), the other guarantor (e.g., Hamid) remains fully liable for the entire debt. The reason is that releasing a guarantor does not release the original debtor (asil/makful anhu). Since the debtor still owes the full amount, and the second guarantor is a guarantor for the debtor (as well as for the first guarantor, but the first is released), the second guarantor's obligation to the creditor remains intact. The creditor can therefore claim the full debt from the unreleased guarantor. This ruling is consistent with the earlier discussion: each guarantor is a guarantor for the debtor and for the co-guarantor. When one is released, his own guarantee is extinguished, but the other's guarantee for the debtor remains. The other guarantor also had a guarantee for the released guarantor, but that is now moot because the released guarantor is no longer liable. However, the passage emphasizes that the unreleased guarantor's liability to the creditor is for the full amount, not just half, because he is a guarantor for the debtor for the whole debt. This is a clear ruling with straightforward reasoning (ta'lil).

Key Terms
ابراء (ibra'): Release; the creditor's act of discharging a debtor or guarantor from liability.
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The creditor; literally 'master of the money'.
اصیل (asil): The principal debtor (makful anhu).

Introduction to Hawalah: Definition, Components, Types, and Proof
Definition and legal analysis of hawalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define hawalah in Hanafi fiqh, identify its four components, distinguish unrestricted and restricted types, and explain its permissibility using the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Hawalah transfers the demand from one debtor to another, discharging the original debtor — a permissible contract supported by hadith.

This lesson introduces the Hanafi treatment of hawalah, a contract for transferring a debt obligation. We will explore its linguistic and technical meaning, the parties involved, the two types (unrestricted and restricted), and the primary evidence for its permissibility from the Sunnah. Understanding hawalah is crucial for distinguishing it from kafalah and for handling debt transfers in Islamic finance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components
Name the four parties involved in a hawalah: muhil (original debtor), muhtal lahu (creditor), muhtal 'alayhi (new debtor), and muhtal bihi (the debt itself). Understanding each role is essential.
The passage lists these terms; note that muhtal bihi is the debt.
2
Distinguish the two types
Explain hawalah mutlaqah (unrestricted) and muqayyadah (restricted) using the example given (Khalid, Hamid, Shahid). Unrestricted has no condition; restricted ties the transfer to a specific debt owed to the muhil.
The example illustrates restricted hawalah.
3
State the Shari'ah definition
Define hawalah as the transfer of the demand from the dhimmah of the debtor to the dhimmah of another. Note the ikhtilaf on whether the debt itself transfers or just the demand.
Some mashayikh hold only demand transfers; others hold both debt and demand transfer.
4
Analyze the evidence for permissibility
Cite the hadith 'Whoever is transferred to a wealthy person should follow him' and the rational argument that the new debtor commits to deliver what he can. The imperative indicates permissibility.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut but accepted as proof for the validity of hawalah.

Source Text

ترجمہ یہ کتاب حوالہ کے بیان میں ہے
كتاب الحواله
تشریح حوالہ اور کفالہ کے درمیان مناسبت یہ ہے کہ ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک عقد التزام نہے یعنی جس طرح کفالہ میں کفیل اپنے اوپر اُس چیز کولازم کرتا ہے جو اصیل پر واجب تھی۔ اسی طرح حوالہ میں محتال علیہ پر وہ چیز لازم کی جاتی ہے جو محیل پر واجب ہوتی ہے اور دونوں کا مقصد توثق اور اعتماد ہے یعنی کفالہ کا مقصد مکفول لہ کو اطمینان دلانا ہے اور حوالہ میں محتال لہ، کو اطمینان دلایا جاتا ہے۔ اور حوالہ چونکہ برات اصیل کو متضمن ہے اس لئے وہ بمنزلہ مرکب کے ہے اور کفالہ چونکہ برأت اصیل کو متضمن نہیں ہوتا اس لئے وہ بمنزلہ مفرد کے ہے اور مفرد چونکہ مرکب پر مقدم ہوتا ہے اس لئے کفالہ کو مقدم اور حوالہ کو مؤخر کیا گیا ہے۔

حوالہ میں چار لفظ بولے جاتے ہیں۔
ا محیل - مدیون
۲ محتال له دائن ، قرض خواہ
-۳ محتال علیہ، وہ شخص جس نے حوالہ قبول کیا ہے۔ محتال به، و هو مال جس کو قبول کیا ہے۔ یعنی نفس دین۔

حوالہ کی شرعی حیثیت
قال وهي جائزة بالديون قال عليه السلام من أحيل على ملئى فليتبع ولانه التزم ما يقدر على تسليمه فتصح
Translation: This book is about hawalah.
Book of Hawalah
Commentary: The connection between hawalah and kafalah is that both are contracts of commitment. Just as in kafalah the surety binds himself to what was obligatory on the principal (asl), similarly in hawalah the new debtor (muhtal 'alayhi) is bound to what was obligatory on the original debtor (muhil). Both aim at reassurance: kafalah aims to reassure the creditor, and hawalah aims to reassure the recipient (muhtal lahu). Since hawalah includes the discharge of the original debtor, it is like a composite, while kafalah does not include discharge, so it is simple. Since the simple precedes the composite, kafalah is placed before hawalah.
Hawalah linguistically denotes removal and transfer; from it is derived 'tahwil' meaning to transfer something from one place to another.
In hawalah, four terms are used:
1. Muhil – the debtor.
2. Muhtal lahu – the creditor.
3. Muhtal 'alayhi – the person who accepts the transfer.
4. Muhtal bihi – that which is transferred, which is the maal, i.e., the debt itself.
In Shari'ah terminology, hawalah is: the transfer of the demand from the dhimmah of the debtor to the dhimmah of a new obligated person (multazim). Then some mashayikh said that after hawalah, the muhil is discharged from both the demand and the debt. Others said he is only discharged from the demand, not the debt.
There are two types of hawalah:
1. Hawalah mutlaqah (unrestricted).
2. Hawalah muqayyadah (restricted).
Unrestricted hawalah is without any condition. It is further divided into two: immediate and deferred.
Restricted hawalah is that the muhil (debtor) transfers the demand to his own debtor. For example, Khalid has 1000 rupees from Hamid, and Hamid has 1500 rupees from Shahid. Then Hamid says to Shahid: 'Give 1000 of my debt to Khalid.' This is called restricted hawalah.
Legal status of hawalah:
He said: It is permissible for debts. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever is transferred to a wealthy person should follow (him).' And because he (the muhtal 'alayhi) has committed himself to something he is able to deliver, so it is valid.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the book of Hawalah. It begins by explaining the relationship between kafalah and hawalah: both are contracts of commitment. Hawalah is like a composite because it includes discharge of the original debtor, while kafalah is simple. Therefore, kafalah is placed before hawalah. Then the linguistic meaning of hawalah is given: transfer. The four components of hawalah are identified: the original debtor (muhil), the creditor (muhtal lahu), the new debtor (muhtal 'alayhi), and the debt (muhtal bihi). The Shari'ah definition is: transfer of the demand from one dhimmah to another. There is a difference among later scholars whether the original debtor is discharged from both the debt and the demand or only from the demand. Then two types are mentioned: unrestricted (which can be immediate or deferred) and restricted (where the transfer is specifically to a person who owes the original debtor). An example is given: Khālid is creditor of Ḥāmid, Ḥāmid is creditor of Shāhid; Ḥāmid transfers his claim from Khālid to Shāhid. Finally, the legal ruling: hawalah is permissible for debts, supported by the hadith 'Whoever is transferred to a wealthy person should follow (him)' and by rational argument that the new debtor commits to deliver what he can.

Key Terms
حوالة (ḥawālah): The transfer of the demand (and possibly the debt) from the original debtor (muhil) to a new debtor (muhtal 'alayhi).
محيل (muḥīl): The original debtor who transfers his debt obligation to another person (muhtal 'alayhi).
محتال عليه (muḥtāl ʿalayhi): The person who accepts the transfer and becomes the new debtor, liable to pay the debt to the creditor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between kafalah and hawalah as explained in the passage?
A) Kafalah is for debts only, hawalah is for goods.
B) Hawalah includes discharge of the original debtor, while kafalah does not.
C) Kafalah requires a surety, hawalah does not.
D) There is no difference; they are the same.

Key Takeaway
Hawalah is a permissible contract for debt transfer that shifts the demand from the original debtor to a new debtor, supported by the hadith 'Whoever is transferred to a wealthy person should follow him.'

The Ruling and Conditions of Hawalah (Transfer of Debt)
Defines hawalah, states its permissibility, and outlines its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hawalah, identify its conditions, and apply the proofs that establish its permissibility.

Core Idea
Hawalah (transfer of debt) is permissible; it requires the consent of creditor and transferee, but not the debtor.

The transfer of debt (hawalah) is a common financial practice that must follow strict legal conditions to be valid. In this passage, al-Quduri presents the ruling, its proof from hadith and reason, and the conditions for validity according to the Hanafi school. Understanding these will help you correctly apply the rules of hawalah in modern financial arrangements.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition and types of hawalah
Hawalah is the transfer of a debt from the original debtor (muhil) to another person (muhtal 'alayh). It can be absolute (without condition) or restricted (to a specific debtor of the muhil).
The text also notes an intra-Hanafi difference on whether the muhil is freed from both the debt and demand or only the demand.
2
Identify the ruling and its primary proof
The matn states that hawalah is permissible for debts. The proof is the hadith: 'Whoever is transferred to a wealthy person should follow (him).' This is a command indicating permissibility according to Hanafi usul (al-amr lil-ibahah).
The hadith is from Sahihayn and Tabarani; it establishes the principle.
3
Analyze the rational proof (qiyas)
The rational proof is that the transferee undertakes what he can deliver, similar to kafalah (guarantee). Since kafalah is valid, hawalah is also valid by analogy.
Qiyas is used to support the textual proof.
4
Determine the conditions for validity
The text lists conditions: consent of the creditor (muhtal) and the transferee (muhtal 'alayh), the debt must be known, and the transferee must be capable of payment. The original debtor's consent is not strictly required.
This is a nuanced Hanafi position – the muhil benefits from discharge, so his consent is not needed.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on the effect of hawalah
Some Hanafi scholars hold that the muhil is freed from both the debt and the demand; others hold he is freed only from the demand. The text records both views without explicit tarjih.
Later preferred view (mufta bihi) is that the muhil is fully discharged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتابُ الحوالة
ترجمہ یہ کتاب حوالہ کے بیان میں ہے
كتاب الحواله
تشریح حوالہ اور کفالہ کے درمیان مناسبت یہ ہے کہ ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک عقد التزام نہے یعنی جس طرح کفالہ میں کفیل اپنے اوپر اُس چیز کولازم کرتا ہے جو اصیل پر واجب تھی۔ اسی طرح حوالہ میں محتال علیہ پر وہ چیز لازم کی جاتی ہے جو محیل پر واجب ہوتی ہے اور دونوں کا مقصد توثق اور اعتماد ہے یعنی کفالہ کا مقصد مکفول لہ کو اطمینان دلانا ہے اور حوالہ میں محتال لہ، کو اطمینان دلایا جاتا ہے۔ اور حوالہ چونکہ برات اصیل کو متضمن ہے اس لئے وہ بمنزلہ مرکب کے ہے اور کفالہ چونکہ برأت اصیل کو متضمن نہیں ہوتا اس لئے وہ بمنزلہ مفرد کے ہے اور مفرد چونکہ مرکب پر مقدم ہوتا ہے اس لئے کفالہ کو مقدم اور حوالہ کو مؤخر کیا گیا ہے۔
حوالہ زوال اور نقل کے معنی پر دلالت کرتا ہے اسی سے تحویل ہے۔ یعنی ایک شئی کو ایک محل سے دوسرے محل کی طرف منتقل کرنا۔
حوالہ میں چار لفظ بولے جاتے ہیں۔
ا محیل - مدیون
۲ محتال له دائن ، قرض خواہ
-۳ محتال علیہ، وہ شخص جس نے حوالہ قبول کیا ہے۔ محتال به، و هو مال جس کو قبول کیا ہے۔ یعنی نفس دین۔
شریعت کی اصطلاح میں حوالہ کہتے ہیں۔ مدیون کے ذمہ سے ملتزم کے ذمہ کی طرف مطالبہ کو منتقل کرنا۔ پھر بعض مشائخ نے کہا کہ حوالہ ہو جانے پر محیل جس طرح مطالبہ سے بری ہو جاتا ہے۔ اسی طرح قرضہ سے بھی بری ہو جاتا ہے۔ اور بعض نے کہا کہ فقط مطالبہ سے بری ہوتا ہے۔ قرضہ سے بری نہیں ہوتا۔ حوالہ کی دو قسمیں ہیں۔
ا حوالہ مطلقہ
۲ حوالہ مقیده
حوالہ مطلقہ جس میں کوئی قید نہ ہو۔ پھر حوالہ مطلقہ کی دو قسمیں ہیں۔
ا۔ میعادی
-۲ غیر میعادی یعنی فی الحال
اور حوالہ مقیدہ یہ ہے کہ میل یعنی مدیون اپنے مدیون کی طرف مطالبہ کو منتقل کر دے۔ مثلاً خالد کے حامد پر ایک ہزار روپیہ ہیں۔ اور حامد کے شاہد پر پندرہ سو روپیہ ہیں۔ پس حامد نے شاہد سے کہا کہ تو میرے قرضہ میں سے ایک ہزار خالد کو دیدے تو یہ حوالہ مقیدہ
کہلائے گا۔
حوالہ کی شرعی حیثیت
قال وهي جائزة بالديون قال عليه السلام من أحيل على ملئى فليتبع ولانه التزم ما يقدر على تسليمه فتصح
كالكفالة وانما اختصت بالديون لانها تنبئ عن النقل والتحويل في الدين لا في العين ترجمہ صاحب قدوری نے کہا اور قرضوں کا حوالہ جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ رسول اکرم صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا کہ وہ شخص جو مالدار پر حوالہ کیا جائے تو وہ اتباع کرے اور اس لئے کہ اس نے ایسی چیز کا التزام کیا ہے جس کے سپرد کرنے پر قادر ہے پس حوالہ صحیح ہے جیسے کفالہ صحیح ہوتا ہے۔ اور حوالہ دیون کے ساتھ خاص کیا گیا۔ اس لئے کہ وہ عقل اور تحویل سے آگاہ کرتا ہے اور تحویل صرف دین میں ہوتی ہے نہ
کہ معین میں ۔
تشریح شیخ قد دری فرماتے ہیں کہ قرضوں کا حوالہ جائز ہے اور جواز پرنفلی اور عقلی دونوں طرح کی دلیلیں موجود ہیں۔ چنا نچ نفتی دلیل ابو ہریرہ کی حدیث ہے۔ صحیحین کے الفاظ یہ ہیں مطل الغنى ظلم و اذا اتبع احد كم على ملئ فلیتبع یعنی مالدار کا ٹال مٹول کرنا ظلم ہے اور جب تم میں سے کسی کو مالدار کے پیچھے ڈالا جائے تو وہ اتباع کرے یعنی مقتال نہ حوالہ قبول کرے۔ اور طبرانی میں یہ حدیث ان الفاظ کے ساتھ ہے مطل الغنی ظلم و من أحيل على ملى فليتبع يعني بالدار کا ٹال مٹول کرنا ظلم ہے اور جس شخص کو مالدار پر حوالہ کیا جائے تو وہ اتباع کرے۔ حاصل یہ کہ اگر مدیون اپنا دین کسی مالدار کی طرف منتقل کرے اور ادائیگی کا ذمہ دار اس کو مقرر کرے تو مالدار کو اور چاہئے کہ وہ ٹال مٹول نہ کرے بلکہ قبول کرلے اور جب مالدار دین کی ادائیگی قبول کرلے تو دائن یعنی محتال یہ بھی قبول کرلے نخرے بازی نہ کرے۔ یہ روایت کم از کم حوالہ کے جواز پر کھلی شہادت ہے۔
عقلی دلیل یہ ہے کہ محتال علیہ نے اپنے اوپر ایسی چیز کو لازم کیا ہے جس کو سپرد کرنے پر قادر ہے۔ اور ایسی چیز کولازم کرنا جس کو سپرد کرنے پر قادر ہے درست ہے۔ اس لئے کفالہ کی طرح حوالہ بھی درست ہوگا۔ رہی یہ بات کہ حوالہ کو دیون کے ساتھ کیوں خاص کیا گیا ہے؟ تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ حوالہ کے معنی نقل اور تحویل کے ہیں۔ اور تحویل دیون کے اندر تو ممکن ہے لیکن اعیان کے اندر ممکن نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ دین غیر متعین ہوتا ہے۔ لہذا اس کو محتال علیہ بھی ادا کر سکتا ہے اور عین چونکہ متعین ہوتا ہے اس لئے اس کو وہی ادا کر سکتا ہے جس کے پاس موجود ہو۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ حوالہ دیون کا جائز ہے اور اعیان کا حوالہ جائز نہیں ہے۔ صاحب کفایہ نے اسی بات کو اپنے علمی انداز میں یوں فرمایا کہ حوالہ جو نقل پر دلالت کرتا ہے اُس سے مراد نقل شرعی ہے اور دین وصف شرعی ہے۔ لہذا انقل شرعی اُس چیز میں مؤثر ہو گا جو شرعا ثابت ہے۔ پس دین جو وصف شرعی ہے اور شرعا ثابت ہے۔ وہ نقل شرعی سے منتقل ہو جائے گا۔ اور رہائین ، تو وہ چونکہ حسی ہے اس لئے نقل شرعی سے منتقل نہ ہوگا بلکہ فضل حسی کی طرف محتاج ہو گا۔ اس سے بھی ثابت ہوا کہ حوالہ دیون میں تو جائز ہے مگر اعیان کا حوالہ جائز نہیں ہے۔
حوالہ محیل محتال لہ اور محتال علیہ کی رضا مندی سے صحیح ہوتا ہے
قال و تصح الحوالة برضاء المحيل والمحتال والمحتال عليه اما المحتال فلان الدين حقه وهو الذي ينتقل بها والذمم متفاوتة فلا بد من رضاه واما المحتال عليه فلانه يلزمه الدين ولا لزوم بدون التزامه واما المحيل فالحوالة تصح بدون رضاه ذكره فى الزيادات لان التزام الدين من المحتال عليه تصرف في حق نفسه وهو لا يتضرر بـ به بل فيه نـ لانه لا يرجع عليه اذا لم يكن بامره
Book of Hawalah (Transfer of Debt). Translation: This is the book concerning hawalah. Explanation: The relationship between hawalah and kafalah is that both are contracts of obligation; just as in kafalah the guarantor makes incumbent upon himself that which was obligatory upon the principal, similarly in hawalah the thing that was obligatory upon the transferor is made incumbent upon the transferee. The purpose of both is to strengthen trust and confidence: in kafalah it is to reassure the beneficiary, and in hawalah it is to reassure the creditor. And since hawalah includes the discharge of the original debtor, it is like a compound, whereas kafalah does not include discharge, so it is like a simple. And since the simple precedes the compound, kafalah is placed before hawalah. Hawalah indicates removal and transfer; from it comes 'tahwil' meaning transferring a thing from one place to another. Four terms are used in hawalah: (1) muhil - the debtor; (2) muhtal lahu - the creditor; (3) muhtal 'alayh - the person who accepts the transfer; (4) muhtal bihi - the property accepted, i.e. the debt itself. In Shari'ah terminology, hawalah is the transfer of the demand from the liability of the debtor to the liability of the person who undertakes it. Then some scholars said that upon hawalah, the muhil is freed from both the demand and the debt; others said he is freed only from the demand, not from the debt. Hawalah is of two types: (1) absolute hawalah (mutlaqah) and (2) restricted hawalah (muqayyadah). Absolute hawalah is that in which there is no condition; it is further divided into two: (a) with a fixed term and (b) without a fixed term, i.e. immediate. Restricted hawalah is when the debtor transfers the demand to his own debtor. For example, Khalid has one thousand rupees due from Hamid, and Hamid has fifteen hundred rupees due from Shahid. Hamid says to Shahid: 'Pay one thousand of my debt to Khalid.' This is called restricted hawalah. The Legal Status of Hawalah: He (al-Quduri) said: 'It is permissible for debts.' The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever is transferred to a wealthy person should follow (him).' And because he has undertaken what he is able to deliver, it is valid like kafalah. It is specifically for debts because it signifies transfer and shifting in debt, not in a specific object. Translation: Al-Quduri said: Transfer of debts is permissible because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever is transferred to a wealthy person should follow (him),' and because he has undertaken a thing he is able to deliver, so hawalah is valid just as kafalah is valid. It is specific to debts because it conveys transfer and shifting, and shifting occurs in debt, not in a specific object. Explanation: Al-Quduri states that hawalah for debts is permissible, and there are both revealed and rational proofs for its permissibility. The revealed proof is the hadith of Abu Hurairah; the wording in the Sahihayn is: 'The procrastination of a wealthy person is injustice, and when one of you is referred to a wealthy person, he should follow (refer).' In Tabarani, the hadith is: 'The procrastination of a wealthy person is injustice, and whoever is transferred to a wealthy person should follow.' The conclusion is that if a debtor transfers his debt to a wealthy person and makes him responsible for payment, the wealthy person should not procrastinate but accept, and when the wealthy person accepts payment, the creditor should also accept and not be difficult. This narration is clear evidence for the permissibility of hawalah. The rational proof is that the muhtal 'alayh has undertaken something he is able to deliver, and undertaking something one is able to deliver is valid. Therefore, hawalah is valid like kafalah. As for why hawalah is specific to debts, the answer is that hawalah means transfer and shifting, and shifting is possible in debts but not in specific objects because debt is unspecified; the muhtal 'alayh can also pay it. A specific object, however, is determined, so only the one who has it can deliver it. Thus it is established that hawalah is permissible for debts but not for specific objects. The author of Kifayah expressed this in his scholarly manner: 'Hawalah indicates transfer; by it is meant legal transfer, and debt is a legal attribute. Therefore legal transfer is effective in that which is legally established. So debt, being a legal attribute and legally established, will be transferred by legal transfer. As for specific objects, they are physical, so they are not transferred by legal transfer but require physical delivery.' This also proves that hawalah is permissible for debts but not for specific objects. Hawalah is valid with the consent of the muhil, the muhtal (creditor), and the muhtal 'alayh. He said: 'Hawalah is valid with the consent of the muhil, the muhtal, and the muhtal 'alayh. As for the muhtal, because the debt is his right and it is being transferred; and liabilities vary, so his consent is necessary. As for the muhtal 'alayh, because the debt is imposed upon him, and imposition cannot occur without his acceptance. As for the muhil, hawalah is valid without his consent, as mentioned in al-Ziyadat, because the undertaking of the debt by the muhtal 'alayh is a disposition regarding his own right, and the muhil is not harmed by it; rather, there is benefit because the muhtal 'alayh does not have recourse against him if it was not by his order.'
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Book of Hawalah. It begins with the definition of hawalah and its relation to kafalah, then explains its types, and finally states its legal ruling and conditions. The author first clarifies that hawalah is the transfer of a debt from the original debtor (muhil) to a new obligor (muhtal 'alayh). Two opinions are mentioned regarding the effect of hawalah: some say the muhil is freed from both the demand and the debt itself; others say only from the demand. The passage then divides hawalah into absolute (mutlaqah, with no condition) and restricted (muqayyadah, where the transfer is to the muhil's own debtor). Absolute hawalah is further subdivided into immediate and deferred. The core ruling, from al-Quduri, is that hawalah is permissible (ja'iz) for debts. The proof is a hadith: 'Whoever is transferred to a wealthy person should follow (him).' This is a command implying permissibility and encouragement. A rational proof is also provided: the muhtal 'alayh undertakes something he can deliver, which is valid by analogy to kafalah. The passage emphasizes that hawalah applies only to debts, not specific physical objects, because debt is an abstract legal attribute that can be transferred by mere agreement, whereas specific objects require physical delivery. The final section, quoting al-Ziyadat, states that the validity of hawalah requires the consent of three parties: the muhil, the muhtal (creditor), and the muhtal 'alayh. However, the muhil's consent is not strictly required; hawalah can be valid without his consent because it is a disposition by the muhtal 'alayh affecting his own liability, and the muhil benefits by being discharged. This is a nuanced Hanafi position, highlighting that the muhtal 'alayh cannot be forced to accept the transfer without his consent, but the muhil's discharge is incidental.

Key Terms
الحوالة (al-ḥawālah): Transfer of debt from the liability of the original debtor to the liability of another person who accepts it.
المحيل (al-muḥīl): The original debtor who transfers the debt.
المحتال له (al-muḥtāl lahu): The creditor whose right is transferred.
المحتال عليه (al-muḥtāl ʿalayh): The person who accepts the transfer and becomes liable to pay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, whose consent is not strictly necessary for the validity of hawalah?
A) The creditor (muhtal lahu)
B) The transferee (muhtal 'alayh)
C) The original debtor (muhil)
D) All three must consent

Key Takeaway
Hawalah is a valid debt transfer instrument in Hanafi fiqh, governed by specific conditions of consent and capability, with the original debtor fully discharged in the preferred view.

Discharge of the Assignor in Hawalah: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of hawalah on the assignor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on the extent of the assignor's discharge in hawalah and identify the mufta bihi position with its proof.

Core Idea
The assignor is discharged from both debt and demand because hawalah linguistically means transfer.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah presents a crucial intra-Hanafi debate about the effect of hawalah (assignment of debt). Does the original debtor (muheel) become completely free from the debt and the creditor's right to demand payment, or only from the demand? The dispute involves Imam Zufar and unnamed Hanafis. Understanding the argument is essential for correctly applying the contract of hawalah in transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Read the first part of the passage and list the three views: (1) discharge from both debt and demand, (2) discharge from demand only, (3) no discharge at all (Imam Zufar).
The first two views are mentioned early; the third is attributed to Imam Zufar.
2
Examine the proof for the first view
The proponents of discharge from both cite a ruling: if the assignee releases the assignor after hawalah, it is invalid; if he releases the assignee-in-debt, it is valid. This shows the debt no longer remains on the assignor.
Focus on the logical link between the validity of ibra' and the location of the debt.
3
Examine the proof for the second view
Those who hold demand-only discharge argue that if the assignee releases the assignee-in-debt, the release is not revoked by the assignee-in-debt's rejection. This indicates the debt was not transferred, because if it had, rejection would revoke the ownership transfer.
They treat ibra' as a form of tamlik (ownership transfer).
4
Analyze Imam Zufar's qiyas and its rebuttal
Imam Zufar analogizes hawalah to kafalah, arguing both are 'contracts of strengthening', so just as the principal remains liable in kafalah, so too does the assignor. The Hanafi response is linguistic: hawalah means transfer, so the debt is moved; kafalah means joining, so the original liability stays.
The rebuttal relies on the principle that Shariah rulings follow literal meanings.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The text declares the first view (discharge from both) as 'sahih qawl'. This is the relied-upon position for fatwa. Note that Imam Zufar's view is rejected, and the second view is also set aside.
Authority: the text is from Zahir al-Riwayah (Ashraf al-Hidayah).

حضرات اس کے قائل ہیں کہ محیل مطالبہ سے بری ہوتا ہے اور قرضہ سے بری نہیں ہوتا۔ اُن کا مستدل یہ مسئلہ ہے کہ اگر محتال لہ نے محتال علیہ کو بری کر دیا تو یہ بری کرنا صحیح ہے۔ اور یہ ابرا محتال علیہ کے رد کرنے سے رد نہیں ہوگا۔ اور اگر قرضہ محتال علیہ کی طرف منتقل ہو جاتا تو محتال علیہ کے رد کرنے سے رد ہو جاتا۔ کیونکہ اس وقت ابراء محتال علیہ کو اُس قرضہ کا مالک کرنا ہوتا جو اس پر ہے اور تملیک رد کرنے سے رد ہو جاتی ہے۔ پس معلوم ہوا کہ قرضہ محتال علیہ کی طرف منتقل نہیں ہوا اور محیل اُس سے بری نہیں ہوا بلکہ فقط مطالبہ سے بری ہوا ہے۔ بہر حال صحیح قول یہی ہے کہ مجیل قرضہ اور مطالبہ دونوں سے بری ہو جاتا ہے۔ اور امام زفر کے نزدیک دونوں سے بری نہیں ہوتا۔ امام زفر نے حوالہ کو کفالہ پر قیاس کیا ہے۔ اور وجہ قیاس یہ ہے کہ کفالہ اور حوالہ دونوں میں سے ہر ایک عقد توثق ہے۔ یعنی مضبوطی کے واسطے کفالہ کی طرح حوالہ بھی کیا جاتا ہے۔ پس جس طرح کفالہ میں اصیل یعنی مکفول عنہ بری نہیں ہوتا اسی طرح حوالہ میں بھی اصیل یعنی مجیل بری نہ ہوگا۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ لغت میں حوالہ منتقل کرنے کے معنی میں آتا ہے۔ اور اس سے حوالہ الغراس، پودہ منتقل کرنے کے معنی میں آتا ہے۔ پس حوالہ کے ذریعہ قرضہ کا منتقل ہونا ضروری ہوا اور قرضہ جب محیل کے ذمہ سے منتقل ہو گیا تو محیل کے ذمہ میں باقی نہ رہے گا۔ اور جب محیل کے ذمہ میں قرضہ باقی نہ رہا تو محیل بری ہو گیا۔ اور رہا کفالہ تو اس کے لغوی معنی ضم (ملانے ) کے آتے ہیں۔ پس کفالہ میں صنم الذمۃ الی الذمہ معتبر ہے۔ اور یہ اصول ثابت شدہ ہے کہ احکام شریعہ اپنے لغوی معنیٰ کے موافق ہوتے ہیں۔ پس حوالہ کے لغوی معنی کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے محیل قرضہ اور مطالبہ سے بری ہو جائے گا۔ اور کفالہ کے لغوی معنیٰ کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے مکفول عنہ پر قرضہ اور مطالبہ باقی رہے گا۔
Ashraf al-Hidayah, volume nine: he is discharged. According to some, he is only discharged from the right to demand, not from the debt. The second disagreement is that of Imam Zufar: he says that the assignor is not discharged from either the debt or the demand. Those who hold that the assignor is discharged from both the debt and the demand base their argument on this issue: if the assignee releases the assignor from the debt after the hawalah, this release is not valid. But if he releases the assignee-in-debt (muhtal 'alayh), the release is valid. So if the debt had remained on the assignor, releasing him would have been valid. From this it is understood that by the hawalah the debt is transferred to the assignee-in-debt and the assignor is discharged from the debt. And those who hold that the assignor is discharged from the demand but not from the debt base their argument on this issue: if the assignee releases the assignee-in-debt, this release is valid, and this release is not revoked by the assignee-in-debt's rejection. If the debt had been transferred to the assignee-in-debt, then his rejection would revoke the release, because then the release would constitute making the assignee-in-debt the owner of the debt that is upon him, and ownership is revoked by rejection. So it is understood that the debt was not transferred to the assignee-in-debt, and the assignor is not discharged from it, but only discharged from the demand. In any case, the correct view is that the assignor is discharged from both the debt and the demand. According to Imam Zufar, he is not discharged from either. Imam Zufar analogizes hawalah to kafalah: both are contracts of strengthening, so just as the principal (makful 'anhu) is not discharged in kafalah, so too the assignor is not discharged in hawalah. Our proof is that in language, hawalah means transfer, and from it comes 'hawalah al-gharas' (transfer of a sapling). So by hawalah, the debt must be transferred; once transferred from the assignor's liability, it no longer remains on him, so he is discharged. As for kafalah, its literal meaning is 'joining', so in kafalah, the joining of one liability to another is considered. And it is an established principle that rulings of Shariah accord with their literal meanings. So, by considering the literal meaning of hawalah, the assignor is discharged from both debt and demand; by considering the literal meaning of kafalah, the principal remains liable for the debt and demand.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of hawalah (assignment of debt) on the assignor (muheel). The central question: once the contract of hawalah is concluded, is the assignor completely freed from the original debt and the creditor's right to demand payment, or is he only freed from the demand while the debt remains on him? Imam Zufar holds that the assignor is not freed from either. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that the assignor is freed from both. The text presents proofs for each view: those who say both freed cite the ruling that if the assignee releases the assignor after hawalah, it is invalid because the debt is no longer on him; but if he releases the assignee-in-debt, it is valid. Those who say only demand freed cite that if the assignee releases the assignee-in-debt, that ibra' is not revoked by rejection, indicating the debt did not transfer. The author then gives the tarjih: the correct view is that both are discharged. Imam Zufar's qiyas to kafalah is rejected based on linguistic analysis: 'hawalah' means transfer, so transfer of debt is inherent; 'kafalah' means joining, so the original liability remains. The Hanafi principle that rulings follow literal meanings is invoked.

Key Terms
حوالہ (hawalah): Transfer of a debt from the liability of the assignor to the liability of the assignee-in-debt.
محیل (muheel): The original debtor who transfers the debt to another.
محتال علیہ (muhtal 'alayh): The person upon whom the debt is assigned (assignee-in-debt).
محتال لہ (muhtal lahu): The creditor (assignee) to whom the debt is owed.
کفالہ (kafalah): Guarantee or surety; a contract where one person guarantees the debt or presence of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the effect of hawalah on the assignor (muheel)?
A) He is discharged from both the debt and the right to demand payment.
B) He is discharged only from the right to demand payment.
C) He is not discharged from either.
D) He is discharged only from the debt.

Key Takeaway
The assignor is completely discharged from both debt and demand after a valid hawalah, based on the literal meaning of 'transfer' and supporting rulings on ibra'.

Tawthiq in Hawalah Despite Assignor's Discharge
Answering objection about tawthiq after assignor discharge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why hawalah provides tawthiq (strengthening) even though the assignor is completely discharged.

Core Idea
Tawthiq in hawalah comes from choosing a more reliable payer, not from retaining dual liability.

In this passage, the author addresses a common objection: if the assignor is completely freed from the debt after hawalah, how can the contract still be considered a strengthening (tawthiq) for the creditor? The answer reveals a key principle: the benefit lies in the substitute's reliability, not in having multiple debtors. This clarifies the nature of hawalah as a transfer of debt, not a guarantee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection claims that if the assignor is discharged, hawalah cannot be tawthiq because there is no dual liability.
Dual liability would mean the creditor can demand from both.
2
State the Hanafi response
The response is that tawthiq does not require dual liability; it is achieved by the substitute's reliability.
3
Analyze the scenarios
Tawthiq is attained when the assignee-in-debt is wealthier or better in repayment conduct than the assignor.
These scenarios make collection more assured.
4
Derive the principle
The value of hawalah lies in the quality of the new debtor, not in retaining the old one.
This is a key Hanafi principle in hawalah.

Source Text

والتوثق باختيار الاملاء .... الخ سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ جب قرضہ محیل سے منتقل ہو کر محتال علیہ پر آ گیا اور محیل بری ہو گیا تو حوالہ، عقد توثق کہاں رہا۔ صرف اتنا ہوا کہ پہلے قرضہ مجیل پر تھا اور حوالہ کے بعد محتال علیہ پر آ گیا ؟ جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ توثق اور مضبوطی کے لئے یہ ضروری نہیں کہ قرضہ دو آدمیوں پر واجب ہو اور قرض خواہ کو دونوں سے مطالبہ کا حق ہو۔ بلکہ برات محیل کے باوجو د توثق حاصل ہو جاتا ہے۔ بایں طور کہ محیل یعنی قرضدار کی بہ نسبت محتال علیہ زیادہ مالدار ہو یا محیل کی بہ نسبت محتال علیہ ادا کرنے میں حسن سلوک کا معاملہ کرتا ہو تو ان دونوں صورتوں میں محیل کی بہ نسبت محتال علیہ سے قرضہ وصول کرنے میں زیادہ وثوق اور مضبوطی ہے۔ پس معلوم ہوا کہ مجیل کی برات کے باوجود بھی حوالہ سے توثق حاصل ہو جاتا ہے۔
And the response to a question from 'al-tawthiq bi-ikhtiyar al-imla'' etc. The question is: When the debt is transferred from the assignor to the assignee-in-debt and the assignor is discharged, where does the hawalah remain a contract of strengthening? It only happened that the debt was previously on the assignor and after hawalah it came onto the assignee-in-debt? The answer: For strengthening, it is not necessary that the debt be owed by two persons and the creditor have the right to demand from both. Rather, strengthening is achieved despite the discharge of the assignor, in that the assignee-in-debt is wealthier than the assignor, or the assignee-in-debt deals with better conduct in repayment. In both these cases, there is more assurance and strength in recovering the debt from the assignee-in-debt compared to the assignor. So it is understood that even with the assignor's discharge, strengthening is obtained from the hawalah.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: if the debt is completely transferred and the assignor is freed, how can hawalah be considered a contract of tawthiq (strengthening)? The answer clarifies that tawthiq does not require dual liability. Rather, the benefit lies in choosing a more reliable party. The assignee (creditor) may agree to the transfer because the assignee-in-debt is more capable of paying or more cooperative. Thus, even though the assignor is discharged, the creditor gains a stronger position. This is a key principle in Hanafi hawalah law.

Key Terms
توثق (tawthuq): Strengthening or securing the debt; making collection more reliable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does hawalah provide tawthiq (strengthening) even though the assignor is completely discharged?
A) The creditor retains the right to demand payment from both the assignor and assignee-in-debt.
B) The assignee-in-debt is wealthier or better in payment than the assignor.
C) The assignor remains liable as a guarantor.
D) The debt is secured by collateral.

Key Takeaway
Hawalah provides tawthiq by transferring the debt to a more reliable payer, not by retaining multiple debtors.

Forced Acceptance of Payment by Assignor: The Rationale
Handling the objection to forced acceptance of payment by assignor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the assignee must accept payment from the assignor after hawalah, using the rational argument that the assignor is not a pure volunteer.

Core Idea
The assignor's payment after hawalah preempts potential loss, so he is not a mutabarri' and the assignee must accept.

This passage addresses an objection: if the assignor pays the debt after hawalah, why is the assignee forced to accept? Critics argue this implies the assignor is still liable. The Hanafi response clarifies that the assignor pays not as charity but to avoid a potential future claim, thus he is not a donor. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
Identify the claim: forcing acceptance implies assignor's continued liability because a pure donor's gift cannot be forced on the recipient.
The objection is based on the definition of mutabarri'.
2
Explain the Hanafi position
The assignor is indeed discharged by hawalah, but there is a possibility of the claim returning (e.g., assignee-in-debt rejects or dies insolvent). This possibility prevents the assignor from being a pure donor.
Discharge is certain, but potential future risk remains.
3
Follow the rational argument (dalil aqli)
A mutabarri' is one who intends charity without intending to remove harm from himself. The assignor pays to eliminate the potential of being pursued, thus he intends to remove harm. Therefore, he is not a mutabarri'.
The key is the intention to remove harm.
4
Rebutt the counter-objection
Some might argue that at the moment of payment, the assignor has no current liability, so he appears to be a volunteer. However, the definition of mutabarri' excludes those who act to prevent future harm, which is exactly what the assignor does.
The potential future harm is real, not fanciful.
5
Conclude with the ruling
Since the assignor is not a mutabarri', his payment is not a mere gift, and the assignee cannot refuse. The assignee is forced to accept, and the payment discharges the assignor's potential future liability.
This ruling protects the assignor and ensures justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وانما يجبر على القبول ..... الخ سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ اگر محیل، محتال لہ کو محتال علیہ کے ادا کرنے سے پہلے قرضہ ادا کر دے تو محتال لہ کو قبول کرنے پر مجبور کیا جاتا ہے۔ اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ قرضہ محیل کے ذمہ باقی ہے کیونکہ اگر محیل کے ذمہ قرضہ باقی نہ ہوتا تو محیل قرض ادا کرنے میں ہوتا۔ اور متبرع کے تبرع کو قبول کرنے پر کسی کو مجبور نہیں کیا جاتا۔ پس محتال لہ کو قبول کرنے پر مجبور کیا جانا اس بات کی علامت ہے کہ محیل متبرع نہیں ہے۔ اور جب محیل متبرع نہیں ہوا تو معلوم ہوا کہ اس کے ذمہ قرضہ باقی ہے اور حوالہ کرنے سے وہ بری نہیں ہوا ہے۔ جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ حوالہ کی وجہ سے قرضہ کا محتال علیہ کی طرف منتقل ہو کر محیل کا بری ہونا تو یقینی ہے لیکن قرضہ کے ہلاک ہونے کی وجہ سے ( بایں طور کہ محتال علیہ حوالہ کا انکار کر دے یا مفلس ہو کر مر جائے ) یہ احتمال ہے کہ مطالبہ محیل کی طرف لوٹ آئے اور جب یہ احتمال ہے تو محیل محض متبرع نہیں ہو گا۔ اور جب متبرع نہیں ہوا تو محتال لہ کو قبول کرنے پر بھی مجبور کیا جائے گا۔ لیکن اگر یہ سوال کیا جائے کہ محیل قرضہ ادا کرتے وقت تو یقینا متبرع ہے کیونکہ فی الحال اس پر کوئی قرضہ نہیں ہے۔ اور قرضہ ہلاک ہونے کی وجہ سے محیل کی طرف مطالبہ کا لوٹ آنا ایک موہوم بات ہے۔ تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ متبرع اس کو کہتے ہیں جو دوسرے پر احسان کا ارادہ کرے اور اس کے ذریعہ اپنے اوپر سے ضرر کو دور کرنا مقصود نہ ہو۔ حالانکہ اس ادا سے یہی مقصود ہے۔ کیونکہ محیل نے قرضہ ادا کر کے اپنے اوپر سے مطالبہ کے امکان کو ساقط کر دیا ہے۔ اور تنگدستی کے وقت قید و جس کے ضرر کو دور کر دیا ہے۔ پس جب اس ادا کرنے میں وفع ضرر بھی مقصود ہے تو محیل متبرع نہیں ہو سکتا۔ اور جب متبرع نہیں ہوا تو محتال لہ کو قبول کرنے پر بھی مجبور کیا جائے گا۔
And the response to a question from 'innama yujbaru 'ala al-qabul' etc. The question is: If the assignor pays the debt to the assignee before the assignee-in-debt pays, then the assignee is forced to accept. From this it is understood that the debt remains on the assignor's liability, because if the debt were no longer on the assignor, he would be paying as a volunteer, and no one is forced to accept a volunteer's charity. So the assignee being forced to accept indicates the assignor is not a volunteer. And when he is not a volunteer, it appears the debt remains on him and he was not discharged by the hawalah. The answer: It is certain that by the hawalah the debt is transferred to the assignee-in-debt and the assignor is discharged. But due to the possibility of loss (e.g., the assignee-in-debt rejects the hawalah or dies insolvent), there is a chance the claim returns to the assignor. Given this possibility, the assignor is not a pure donor. And since he is not a donor, the assignee is forced to accept. But if one objects that at the time of payment, the assignor is certainly a donor since currently no debt is on him, and the return of claim is fanciful, the answer is: A donor is one who intends charity and does not intend to remove harm from himself. Here, the assignor does intend to remove harm: by paying, he eliminates the potential of being pursued for the debt and avoids the harm of imprisonment in times of hardship. Since repelling harm is also intended, he cannot be a donor. And when he is not a donor, the assignee is forced to accept.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage deals with an objection: if the assignor pays the debt after hawalah (but before the assignee-in-debt pays), the assignee is forced to accept. Objectors argue this proves the debt still rests on the assignor. The Hanafi response is that the assignor is indeed discharged, but the possibility of the claim returning in case of loss (e.g., assignee-in-debt rejects or becomes insolvent) prevents him from being a pure volunteer. The assignor pays to preempt a future risk, not as a gift. Therefore, the assignee must accept. This clarifies the Hanafi principle that even after discharge, the assignor may still have an interest in paying to avoid potential future liability.

Key Terms
متبرع (mutabarri'): A person who gives something voluntarily without any intention of removing a harm from themselves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in the passage, why is the assignee forced to accept the assignor's payment after hawalah?
A) Because the assignor is still legally the debtor.
B) Because the assignor is a volunteer and charity must be accepted.
C) Because the assignor pays to avoid potential future harm, so he is not a pure donor.
D) Because the hawalah contract is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The assignor's payment after hawalah is not a gift; it is a preemptive act to avoid potential harm, so the assignee must accept.

Assignee's Recourse to Assignor: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Stating the ruling and dispute on assignee's recourse upon loss of right.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the assignee's right of recourse upon loss of his right, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position using the qiyas to the attribute of safety in a sale.

Core Idea
The assignee can revert to the assignor if the assignee-in-debt fails, because the discharge is conditional on safety of the right.

This lesson examines a key inter-madhhab dispute in the chapter of hawalah (transfer of debt). After a valid hawalah, the assignor is normally discharged. But what happens if the assignee's right is lost—say the assignee-in-debt rejects, dies, or becomes insolvent? The Hanafi school allows the assignee to return to the original assignor, while Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad hold the discharge is absolute and irrevocable. We'll analyze both proofs and the underlying legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the dispute
Read the first part: the author states the default rule (no recourse unless right is lost), then the dispute with al-Shafi'i. Note the key term 'tawayya haqqahu' (loss of right).
The default rule is that the assignee cannot recourse; the exception is loss of right.
2
Extract the Hanafi proof
Look for the phrase 'Lana annaha muqayyadah bi-salamat haqqihi' (our proof: the discharge is conditional on safety of his right). Also note the alternative: 'aw tafsakh al-hawalah' (or the hawalah is rescinded). Understand the analogy to the attribute of safety in a sale.
The discharge is not absolute; it is implicitly conditional.
3
Extract the Shafi'i proof
Find the Shafi'i argument: 'al-bara'ah qad thalabat mutlaqatan' (the discharge has been absolutely established). Therefore, the debt cannot return except by a new cause.
Absolute discharge means no conditions attached.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response (qiyas)
The text says the hawalah contract is subject to rescission (qabil lil-faskh), like the attribute of safety in a sale. Map the analogy: asl: safety of sold item → ruling: option of return (khiyar al-ayb). far: safety of assignee's right. illa: failure of the purpose (fawt al-maqsud).
This is qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Hanafi law, if the assignee-in-debt becomes insolvent, the assignee can still claim from the assignor. Under Shafi'i law, the assignee bears the risk and has no recourse. This affects real estate and financial transactions.
The practical difference is the risk allocation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محتال کو محیل پر رجوع حق ہے یا نہیں
قال ولا يرجع المحتال على المحيل الا ان يتوى حقه و قال الشافعي لا يرجع وان توى لان البراءة قد فلا يعود الابسبب جديد ولنا انها مقيدة بسلامة حقه له اذ هو المقصود او تفسح الحوالة حصلت مطلقه لفواته لانه قابل للفسخ فصار كوصف السلامة في المبيع
ترجمہ اور محتال لہ کو محیل سے رجوع کا حق نہیں ہوگا۔ مگر یہ کہ اس کا حق ہلاک ہو جائے اور امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ رجوع نہیں کرے گا اگر چہ ہلاک ہو جائے کیونکہ محیل کا بری ہونا مطلقاً ثابت ہے لہذا قرضہ نہیں عود کرے گا۔ مگر سبب جدید کے ساتھ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ برات اس کے حق کی سلامتی کے ساتھ مقید ہے اس لئے کہ یہی مقصود ہے۔ یا مقصود فوت ہونے سے حوالہ مسخ ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ عقد حوالہ، سیخ کو قبول کرنے والا ہے۔ پس ایسا ہو گیا جیسے مبیع میں سلامتی کا وصف۔
تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ حوالہ مکمل ہونے کے بعد محتال لہ کو محیل سے رجوع کا اختیار نہیں ہوتا۔ ہاں اگر اس کا حق تلف ہو جائے مثلاً محتال علیہ حوالہ کا انکار کر دے یا مر جائے یا حاکم اُس کے مفلس ہونے کا اعلان کر دے تو اس صورت میں محتال لہ، محیل سے رجوع کر سکتا ہے۔ اور حضرت امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ محتال لہ کا حق تلف ہونے کے باوجود محتال لہ کو محیل سے رجوع کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ اس کے قائل امام احمد ہیں۔
امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ محیل کا بری ہونا مطلقا ثابت ہے اس میں یہ کوئی قید نہیں کہ اگر محتال لہ کا حق تلف ہونا ہو تو بری نہیں ہو گا۔ بلکہ اس سے محتال لہ کو رجوع کا حق ہو گا۔ بہر حال جب محیل کا بری ہونا مطلقاً ثابت ہے تو محیل پر قرضہ عود نہیں کرے گا۔ الا یہ کہ کوئی
Does the assignee have the right to recourse against the assignor or not? He (the author) said: The assignee does not have recourse against the assignor unless his right is lost. Al-Shafi'i said: He does not have recourse even if it is lost, because the assignor's discharge is absolute, so the debt does not return except by a new cause. Our proof: The discharge is conditional upon the safety of his right, because that is the purpose. Or, the hawalah becomes annulled due to the failure of its purpose, because it is subject to rescission. So it becomes like the attribute of safety in the sold item. Translation: The assignee does not have the right of recourse against the assignor unless his right is lost. Imam al-Shafi'i said: He will not have recourse even if it is lost, because the assignor's discharge is absolutely established, so the debt will not return except through a new cause. Our proof: The discharge is conditional upon the safety of his right, because that is the purpose; or the hawalah becomes rescinded due to the failure of its purpose, because the contract of hawalah is subject to rescission. So it becomes like the attribute of safety in the sold item. Elaboration: The issue is that after the completion of hawalah, the assignee does not have the option to recourse against the assignor. However, if his right is lost, e.g., the assignee-in-debt rejects the hawalah, or dies, or the judge declares his insolvency, then the assignee can recourse against the assignor. Imam al-Shafi'i said that even if his right is lost, the assignee will not have recourse. This is also the view of Imam Ahmad. Imam al-Shafi'i's proof: The assignor's discharge is absolute; there is no condition in it that if the assignee's right is lost, he is not discharged. In any case, when the assignor's discharge is absolutely established, the debt will not return to him unless by a new cause.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage addresses the rule of the assignee's right of recourse to the assignor after a valid hawalah. The Hanafi position: the assignee normally cannot revert to the assignor. However, if the assignee's right is frustrated (e.g., the assignee-in-debt rejects, dies, or is declared insolvent), then recourse is allowed. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that no recourse is possible even in that situation, because the assignor's discharge is absolute. The Hanafi proof: the discharge is implicitly conditional on the safety of the assignee's right; if that safety is lost, the hawalah is effectively rescinded, analogizing to the attribute of safety in a sale (if the sold item is defective, the buyer may return it). The text also notes that Imam Ahmad shares al-Shafi'i's view. The passage ends abruptly, likely continuing with further explanation.

Key Terms
توی حق (tawayya haqquh): The loss or destruction of the assignee's right (e.g., assignee-in-death dies insolvent, rejects, etc.).
برائت (bara'ah): The discharge of the assignor from the debt.
حوالہ (hawalah): Transfer of debt from one debtor to another, or from debtor to a third party who then becomes the creditor's new debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what triggers the assignee's right to recourse against the assignor after a valid hawalah?
A) The assignor's request for payment.
B) The loss of the assignee's right (e.g., assignee-in-debt rejects, dies, or insolvent).
C) The lapse of one year.
D) The assignee's payment of a fee.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the assignor's discharge in hawalah is conditional upon the safety of the assignee's right; if that right is lost, the assignee may revert to the assignor.

Definition of Tawa: Dispute Between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the definition of tawa (insolvency) in hawalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two situations that constitute tawa according to Abu Hanifa and explain why the Sahibayn add a third situation.

Core Idea
Tawa is certain inability to collect; Abu Hanifa limits it to denial with oath or death, but the Sahibayn include a judge's insolvency declaration.

In hawalah (debt transfer), the creditor's right to revert to the original debtor depends on 'tawa'—the inability to collect from the transferee. This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute: does a judge's declaration of insolvency constitute tawa? Understanding this difference is essential for correctly applying the rules of recourse in hawalah.

Guided Path
1
Define tawa in context
Tawa means the creditor's inability to collect the debt from the muhal 'alayh (transferee), allowing reversion to the muhil (transferor). Identify the legal consequence of tawa.
2
State Abu Hanifa's two situations
List the two cases: (1) denial with oath and no evidence, (2) death insolvent without property or debts.
These guarantee permanent inability.
3
State the Sahibayn's addition
Explain that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad add a third case: the judge rules the muhal 'alayh insolvent during his lifetime.
They consider the court's declaration legally binding.
4
Identify the underlying principle
Abu Hanifa argues wealth is transient (ghadi wa ra'ih), so a court order does not guarantee permanent inability; the Sahibayn give the court's ruling decisive effect.
This is based on a rational (dalil aqli) argument.
5
Note the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, the creditor cannot revert upon a court insolvency declaration alone; under the Sahibayn, he can.
The mufta bihi is not specified in this passage.
